From these passages it is clear that Apastamba had before him a Pur─Бс╣Зa called Bhaviс╣гyat and also that the Purana or Pur─Бс╣Зas which he knew contained rules about the food to be accepted for eating, about the stages of householder and the perpetual student, about resisting an ─Бtat─Бyin oven unto death and about creation and re-creation after dissolution. These matters fall within the province of Smrtis and Pur─Бс╣Зas. As the word ‘pur─Бс╣Зa’ means ‘ancient’, the word Bhaviс╣гyat-pur─Бс╣Зa is a contradiotion in terms. Long before ─Аpastamba the word Pur─Бс╣Зa had come to mean a work dealing with ancient tales &o; several such works must have been composed and they probably began to incorporate contem porary events and wrote about such events in a prophetio vein. Hence arose the name Bhaviс╣гyat-pur─Бс╣Зa. 2329 As Apastambe mentions a Bhaviс╣гyat-pur─Бс╣Зa and also Pur─Бс╣Зa, it follows that before 500 B. C. several Pur─Бpas existed one of which was called the Bhaviс╣гyat and the Pur─Бс╣Зas then known contained the topics of sarga, pratisarga and smrti matters.

This conclusion is strongly corroborated by other facts. The Gautama-dharmas┼лtra provides that a learned (bahusruta) brahmana is one who knows peoples’ usages, the Veda, the angas (auxiliary lor├лs), V─Бkov─Бkya (dialogues), Itih─Бsa and Pur─Бс╣Зa and that the king in administering the affairs of his kingdom and justice has to depend upon the Veda, Dharmasastra, the (six) angas of the Veda, the (four) Upavadas and Pur─Бс╣Зa. 1330

Br., Tai. ─Аr. and the Upaniс╣гads, by the time of ─Аp. and Gautama, Pur─Бpas approaching in contents to some extent at least some of the extant Pur─Бс╣Зas had come into existence. The

B It is impossible to hold that all the numerous references to Puranas are later interpolations, though a few may be go. It is not correct to say that no Pur─Бс╣Зa resembling the extant ones existed before the time when Pur─Бс╣Зa stories were collected in the Maha bharata. There is hardly any evidence to support any such view.

Early Sanskrit authors like Bapa (first half of 7th century A D.) and commentators like Sabara (not later than between 200-400 A. D.), Kum─Бrila (7th century A. History of Dharma┼Ы─Бstra (Soo. IV, Ch. XXII

Pur─Бс╣Зas 1345 it is narrated that in the Kali age there will arise ┼Ъakya (Gautama Buddha) and others that will cause confusion about dharma; who would listen to their worda ?’ This shows that before the 7th century A. D. the Pur─Бс╣Зas contained descrip tions of the nature of Kaliyuga and that the Pur─Бс╣Зas Kum─Бrila knew did not regard Buddha as an avat─Бra of Viс╣гс╣Зu, but rather condemned him. From the facts that Kс╣гemendra composed his Da┼Ы─Бvat─Бracarita in 1066 A. D., that Apar─Бrka (on p. 338 ) quotes a long passage from the Matsya chapter 285, verse seven of which enumerates the ton avat─Бras of Viс╣гou (including Buddha), and that the Gitagovinda of Jayadeva regards Buddha as an avat─Бra, it follows that before 1000 A. D. Buddha bad come to be regarded as an avat─Бra of Viс╣гс╣Зu, though before the 7th century some Pur─Бс╣Зas at least had condemned him. In the discussion of the meaning of the word ‘svarga’ 134 Kum─Бrila asks: does it mean the region of the stars or does it mean the top of the Meru (mountain) in conformity with Itih─Бsa and Pur─Бс╣Зas or does it mean only a state of happiness? This indicates that in Kum─Бrila’s day Pur─Бс╣Зas contained descriptions of the top of Moru as ‘heaven.’

Saс╣Еkar─Бc─Бrya in his bh─Бsya on the Ved─Бntas┼лtra frequently mentions the contents and characteristic features of Pur─Бс╣Зas that are the same as those of the extant ones, though he actually names none of them. For example, on V.S. IL 1. 36 he remarks that it has been established in the Pur─Бс╣Зa that there is no limit to the number of past and futuro 1347 kalpas. On V. 8. I. 3.30.

From the foregoing discussion about the references to Puranas contained in writers from Sabara to Vi┼Ыvar┼лpa it follows that between about the 2nd century AD, and the 6th or 7th century AD. the Pur─Бс╣Зas exhibited the same contents and characteristics as many of the extant Pur─Бс╣Зas do.

Before proceeding further, it is necessary to say here some thing about the Yuga-pur─Бс╣Зa, a part of the G─Бrgi-Samhit─Б, and one of the earliest extant works bearing the title Pur─Бс╣Зas. Kern in his Introduction to the Bс╣нhatsambit─Б (pp. 32-40 ) brought this rare Pur─Бс╣Зa containing valuable historical data to the notice of scholars from a fragmentary ms. Later on K. P. Jayaswal 1348, published a text of the purely historical material of the Yugapur─Бс╣Зa in 115 half lines in Anustubh metre based on the fragment that Kern had got and two more mss. that were secured by him and added a translation and notes. Later on Jayaswal obtained a copy made by Prof. Levi from a ms, (in Bengali script) in the Bibliotheque Nationale of Paris and published in JBORS. (vol. XV pp. 129-133) a table comparing the readings of that ms with the text he published in JBORS. vol. XIV. Prof. K. H. Dhruva contributed a paper on the text of the Yugapur─Бс╣Зa to the JBORS. vol. XVI. pp. 18-66, wherein he altered the proper names and gave free scope to his con jectures and inforences with the result that succeeding scholars have not attached any weight to his emendations and remarks, Prof. D. Pancalas and Mathuras will attack S─Бketa and will conquer Kusumapura which had a mud fortification. All countries will be disturbed (by this onslaught of the Yavanas). Then non Aryans will follow the practices of the Aryas. At the end of Kali age br─Бhmaс╣Зas, ksatriyas, vai┼Ыyas will dress alike and have the same practices. Thereafter the greedy Saka king will undergo destruction against Sata, the king of Kalinga, and the earth will be devast ated and Puspapura will be a wilderness. Aml─Бta called ‘red. eyed’ will secure Puс╣гpapura. The Mleccha king Aml─Бta will destroy the helpless people and the four varс╣Зas. Aml─Бta with his relatives will perish and then there will be a king called Gopala who will rule one year and then die. There will then be the just king called Pusyaka who will rule only one year. After two more kings, Agnimitra will be king who will wage a terrible battle with br─Бhmapas for a girl. After him his son will rule for 20 years. The condition of the people will be very bad because of his fight with Sabarag. Then S─Бtu king will rule. Then there will be depredations of Sakas who will destroy One-fourth of the population and make the people demoralised, In this way the Yugapur─Бpa ends on a dismal note.1348 e

As the Purana stops with the Sakas and does not dilate on the dynasties of the Andhras, Abhiras and Guptas, it must be placed earlier than all the known Pur─Бс╣Зas which deal with these dynasties. Jayaswal places the Yugapur─Бс╣Зa in the latter half of the first century B. C. In the opinion of the present author he is right.

Several Puranas such as Matsya 53.3-11, V─Бyu 1.60-61, Brahm─Бс╣Зda I 1.40-41, Liiga I. 2.2, N─Бradiya I 92.22-26, Padma V. 1. 45-52 state that Purana 138 was originally only one, that Brahm─Б thought of it first, that after that the Vedas proceeded from Brahma’s lips, that originally it had the extent of one hundred crores of ┼Ыlokas and that the essence thereof to the e tent of four lakhs of verses was declared in each Dv─Бpara age by Vy─Бsa. It is impossible to say whether the writers of the entant Pur─Бс╣Зas had any ancient tradition about this before them a whether all this about the original existence of a single Parapa was purely imaginary. The author bolds that the latter vier

is more likely to be the correct one. It has been shown above that as early as the Taittiriya ─Аrapyaka Pur─Бс╣Зas are mentioned in the plural. Therefore, the extant Puranas are only the successors of the ancient Puranas, about which, it must be admitted, we know very little.

The number of Puranas (in later times and by some of the Puranas distinguished as Mab─Бpuradas) has been traditionally handed down as eighteen. They are enumarated in several Puranas, such as Vispu III. 6. 21-23, Var─Бha 112,69-72 ( Yerses 74-77 in Venk. ed.), Linga I. 39. 61-63, Mataya 53.11 , Padus IV. 100. 51-54, Bhaviс╣гya I L 61-64. Notices of the contents of all the 18 Puranas occur in Matsya ( chap. 53 ), Agni (chap. 272), Skanda (Prabh─Бsakhanda 2. 28-76), N─Бradiya (which devotes 18 chapters from I 92. 30-43 to I. 109 to the contents of 18 Pur─Бс╣Зas from Brahma to Brahm─Бnda ). There is almost complete agreement among the Pur─Бpas as to the names of the 18 principal Pur─Бс╣Зas, except as to the V─Бyupur─Бс╣Зa.

The question about the Pur─Бс╣Зas is further complicated by the fact that some of the Pur─Бpas themselves mention the names of a number of Upapur─Бс╣Зas, though others ignore them. For example, the Matsya ( 53. 59-62 ) names the Narasimha, the Nandi, the Aditya and Samba as Upapur─Бс╣Зas and appears to say that the Narasimha extended to 18000 ┼Ыlokas and elaborated the description of the Man-Lion avat─Бra declared in the Padma pur─Бс╣Зa. The K┼лrma (I. 1. 16-20 ), Padma (IV. 111. 95-98 ), the Devibhagavata (I. 3. 13-16) set out the names of eighteen Upapur─Бс╣Зas. Some of the Upapur─Бс╣Зas bear the same names as those of the principal pur─Бс╣Зas, viz. Skanda, V─Бmana, Brahm─Бnda and N─Бradiya. Prof. Hazra 1358 states that the number of Upa pur─Бс╣Зas is 100. Only a few of the Upapur─Бс╣Зas have been published and those published do not differ much in the matter of subjects from some of the principal pur─Бс╣Зas and almost all belie the definition of Pur─Бс╣Зa as pancalaksana’. It has been seen above ( note 1349 ) that the number of slokas in the 18 principal Puranas is said to be four lakhs. It must be remem bered that this total does not include the number of the slokas assigned to the Upapur─Бс╣Зas, and no Pur─Бpa includes the perses of the Upapur─Бс╣Зas in the total of four lakhs. Matsya and K─Бrma about the Upapuripal. The Matsya 1389 speaks of the Upapur─Бс╣Зas da sub-sections (upabhedas) of the principal eighteen Pur─Бс╣Зas and emphatically asserta ‘know that whatever is declared as distinct from the eighteen Puranag came forth ( or issued from ) these (18). The K┼лrma is no less clear. It states that 1360 the Upapur─Бс╣Зas are the summaries or abridgments of the 18 (principal) Puranas made by sages after studying them. The lists of Upapur─Бс╣Зas given by several works, most of which are set out by Prof. Hasra in his paper on Upapar─Бgas (in ABORI vol. XXI at pp. 40-48) and in his Studies (pp. 4-13) diverge a good deal from each other. As the Matsya mentions by name only four Upapur─Бgas it is not unreasonable to hold that more than four were not in existence at the time when this passage of the Matsys was inserted in it or at least that more than four bad not been accepted as Upapar─Бnas at that time. History of Dharma┼Ы─Бstra (Soc. IV, Oh. XXII dharmottara, the Dovl, can possibly be held to be as old as the 7th or 8th century A.D. I do not accept the view of Prof. Hazra, who places the period of the formation of the group of eighteen Upapur─Бс╣Зas between 650-800 A.D. (in ABORI. vol. XXI at p.50 and also in’ Studies in Upapur─Бс╣Зas ‘vol. I. by Dr. Hazra ), where while admitting (p. 14 ) that in the Upapur─Бpa litarature there are works of comparatively late dates, be boldly states that the age of the Upapur─Бс╣Зas began approximately from the Gupta period (p. 16). For this last statement there is absolutely no evidence whatever. A detailed discussion of the dates of all the Upapur─Бс╣Зas would demand a large space and would be some what irrelevant in this section. We must hold fast by the facts that even when the 18 principal pur─Бс╣Зas assumed their present form, the number of Upapur─Бpas was small, that they were looked upon as no more than abridgments or sum maries of the principal Pur─Бс╣Зas, that the Puranas that mention upapur─Бpas do not state that they were composed by the divinely inspired Vy─Бsa (but only by some bages as the K┼лrma says) and that originally they had not the status or authority of the 18 pur─Бс╣Зas. The Saura, an Upapur─Бс╣Зa itself, speaks of Upapu r─Бnas as kchilas (9.5). It is only the late medieval digesta like the Smrtitattva (1520тАУ1570 A. D.) or the Viramitrodaya (first half of 17th century) that were separated by several centuries from the Mahapur─Бс╣Зas as well as the Upapur─Бс╣Зas and the writers of which had practically lost all sense of the distance in time between the two sets of Pur─Бс╣Зas, that say that the mention of Pur─Бс╣Зas 1361 as a source of dharma in Y─Бj. must be deemed to include Upapur─Бс╣Зas also. This last may be the opinion of such late writers as Mitrami┼Ыra, but no scholar is bound to accept it. It is doubtful whether by the word Pur─Бс╣Зa, Y─Бj. refers to the Mah─Бpur─Бс╣Зas now extant and whether he knows that there were eighteen of them. If some Upapur─Бс╣Зas glorify themselves as of equal authority with the Mah─Бpur─Бс╣Зas, that is of a piece with what the principal Pur─Бс╣Зas state about themselves viz. that Brahm─Б first thought of the Pur─Бnes and then the Vedas issued from his lips. Modern scholars should discount all these attempts at self-glorification. Upapur─Бс╣Зas are due to sages. Upapur─Бpas are distinguished from Pur─Бс╣Зas in several important respecta. History of Dharmal─Бstra (sec. IV, Oh. XX1

them naturally lead to the inference that these passages were added some time after all the eighteen Pur─Бс╣Зas segumed & complete form. It is not possible to hold that all the main pur─Бс╣Зas were composed at the same time by one individual author or even by several contemporary writers. Besides, most of the editions of Puranas are based either on a single ms. or on a fow mss. collected at random and are not critically edited as is the case with the critical edition of the Mahabh─Бrata published in Poona by the B.O.R. I. Many conclusions there. fore, drawn from the current printed editions of the Pur─Бс╣Зas or from msg. of the Puranas, must be regarded as merely tentative and as likely to turn out to be wrong. What Winternitz said in his History of Indian Literature (English translation, Calcutta, vol. I. p. 469) viz ’that the date of each section, day, sometimes of each single verse in the Mab─Бbh─Бrata must be determined separately applies with equal (or perhaps greater) force to the Pur─Бс╣Зas, perticularly when one wants to use the section or stanza for historical or comparative purposes.1363

It may be conceded that the Puranas and some of the avail. able Upapur─Бс╣Зas also contain many ancient legends and tradi tions; but these have been so much tampered with and inflated by additions intended to bolster up particular forms of worship and particular tenets that great caution is required before one can recognize them as genuine and reliable representatives for ascertaining the general state of Indian society and beliefs in ancient and medieval times.

In the opinion of the present writer there is no positive objective evidence for placing any of the upapur─Бс╣Зas except the Viс╣гс╣Зudbarmottara before the 8th or 9th century A. D. Even in the case of Pur─Бс╣Зas, there have been large interpolations such as verses about the 18 pur─Бс╣Зas, their number and contents. But they contain much ancient material and are far more reliable than most of the Upapur─Бс╣Зas, having been quoted by writers from 8th and 9th centuries A, D. Or even earlier.

manvantaras, vaidyacarita, the expanse of the world, rulos about danas, ┼Ыr─Бddha, varс╣Зas and a┼Ыramas, ista and purta, establishment of images of gods, and everything else.

It is not quite clear why the Amarako┼Ыa seized upon the above mentioned five topics as characteristics of Pur─Бpas Amarako┼Ыa 1367 cannot be placed later than the 5th century A. D. It is likely that before that time the number of Pur─Бpas was not large, that they had not become very inflated, and that, as Itih─Бsa and Pur─Бс╣Зa were often lumped together as the 5th Veda in the Upaniс╣гads, they both bad certain matters in common. Itihasa did not probably deal with creation, dissolution and manvastaras, but contented itself with the dynasties of kings and with the deeds and legends about the heroes of the past. Rarely, Itih─Бsa (Mah─Бbh─Бrata) is called Pur─Бс╣Зa and some extant Pur─Бс╣Зas describe themselves as Itib─Бsa. For example, the V─Бgu 1368 calls itself in the same context both itib─Бsa and pur─Бс╣Зa. The Brabmapur─Бс╣Зa calls itself Pur─Бс╣Зa as well as ─Бkhy─Бna (245, 27 and 30). The Mah─Бbh─Бrata 1369, though generally called itib─Бsa by itaelf (as in Adi 1. 19, 26, 54 ) or the best of itih─Бsas, still applies to itself the words. ‘─Бkhy─Бna 1370 (as in Adi 2. 388 89), Kavya (Adi 2. 390), K─Бrс╣гpaveda (─Аdi 1. 264) and Purana (Adi 1. 17). From this it appears that originally the line of demarcation between the two was rather thin. lo defining Pur─Бpa as ‘pa├▒calaksana’ the Amarako┼Ыa and some of the Pur─Бс╣Зas seized upon such topics as distinguished Pur─Бс╣Зas from Itih─Бsa and other branches of Sanskrit literature. What Pur┼лnas contained in Apostamba’s day 841 shown above that the Purana and Bhaviс╣гyatpur─Бс╣Зa that existed before ─Аpastamba contained not only sarga and pratisarga but . also some smrti materials as well. From the definition given in the Pur─Бс╣Зas and the Amarako┼Ыa it does not at all follow that those five topics alone were the constituent parts of the very ancient Pur─Бс╣Зas, as Kirfel holds; 1371 What could be said at the most is that five were the distinctive topics that differentiated the Pur─Бpa class of works from other branches of literature and probably cognate works called Itihasa or it may be that the five are generalized as an ideal for Puranas and that the very early representatives of this class (that existed before Ap. Dh. 8.) had not these five as characteristics.

The extant Puranas contain far more subjects than the five. Some Pur─Бс╣Зas barely touch these five and deal at great length with altogether different topics. Only a few of the extant Pur─Бpas can be said to deal with all the five topics at some length. The five characteristic topics occupy less than three percent of the extent of the extant Mah─Бpur─Бс╣Зas. Of all Pur─Бс╣Зas the extant Viс╣гс╣Зu alone closely agrees with the definition of Pur─Бс╣Зa as ‘pa├▒oalaksaс╣Зa,’ though it also contains & good many other topics. On a modest calculation the four subjects of vrata, ┼Ыr─Бddha, tirtha and d─Бpa cover at least one hundred thousand slokas in the extant eighteen main Puranas. Several of them have identical chapters on several topics (e. g. Matsya 572 and Padmaa, V─Бyu and Brahminda contain long passages that agree verbatim with one another in many matters and details). It is probable, therefore, that the extant principal Pur─Бс╣Зas are partial and gradually inflated representatives of an earlier group of Puranas (not necessarily 18 in number) that

existed before Y─Бj├▒avalkya. In the present state of our know ledge it is almost an insoluble problem to find out what these Pur─Бс╣Зas were or contained. The number 18 was probably due to the fact that the number is prominent in several connections as regards the Mah─Бbh─Бrata. The Bharata war was fought for 18 days, the total of the vast armies engaged in the conflict came to 18 akс╣гauhiс╣Зss, the epic has 18 purvans, the Gita also has 18 chapters.1373

The Pur─Бс╣Зas may be classified into several categories, viz. (1) encyclopaedic like the Agni, Garuс╕Нa and N─Бradiya, (2) those mainly dealing with tirthas such as Padma, Skanda and Bhaviс╣гya, (3) sectarian, such as Linga, V─Бmana, M─Бrkandeya, (4) Historical such as V─Бyu and Brabmanda. The Vayu, Brahm─Бnda, Matsya and Viс╣гс╣Зu are probably the oldest among extant Pur─Бpas, though they too have received substantial additions from time to time.

There are seven Pur─Бс╣Зas that contain historical material, viz, the ancient dynasties down to the time of the Bh─Бrata war and from the Bharata war to the downfall of the Andhras and the rise of the Guptas, 1974 viz. V─Бyu 99. 250-435, Viс╣гс╣Зu IV. 20. 12 to IV. 24. 44, Brahm─Бnda III, 74, 104-248, Bhagavata IX. 12. 9-16, IX. 22. 34-49 and XII. 17, Garuс╕Нa 140 and 141. 1-12, Bhaviс╣гya III. 3 and 4 (this account is practically worthless). Some remarks on the important conclusions of Pargitor and Kirfel are necessary, Pargiter tries to construct history from the earliest times to the Bh─Бrata war which he holds to have taken place about 950 B. O. (AIHT, chap. 15 p. 182). He holds that there were two traditions in ancient India, viz. the Ksatriya tradition and the Br─Бhmaс╣Зa tradition (AIHT, chap. 5 pp. 58-77). He harps dozens of times on the utter lack of the historio sense among the br─Бhmaс╣Зas in his work (AIHT), holds that the Pur─Бс╣Зas represent the ksatriya tradition, that there were three racial stocks, viz. the M─Бnavas (or M─Бovas as he styles them), the Ailas and the Saudyumnas, that respectively represent the Dravidian, the Aryan and Munda (AIHT chap, 25 pp. 289тАУ302), that the Puranas are Sanskritizations of works in Prakrit (pp. X-XI ) of ‘Dynasties of the Kali age’. His date for the Mah─Бbh─Бrata war has not been accepted by later scholars, since his handling of that subject is not judioial, objective or straightforward, but relies too much on his own prejudices and on averages. In his ‘Purana texts of the dynasties of the Kali age (pp. 58, 74) he appears to favour the view that the Bh─Бrata war was fought 1050 years before the Nandas i. e, about 1475 B.C. the crowning of Nanda, viz, 1015 years (Viс╣гс╣Зu), 1050 years (V─Бyu, Brahm─Бnda and mss. of Matsya), 1115 years ( Bhagavata ), 1500 years (some mss. of Viс╣гс╣Зu and of Matsya). Pargiter himself argues forcefully for the trustworthiness of tradition and Paur─Бnika genealogies in R.G. Bhandarkar Presentation volume pp. 107тАУ113 and in AIHT chap. X. p. 119тАУ125. Besides, there is the com mon experience that the total number of years between one well known event and another can be easily remembered and handed down even orally for hundreds of years, while handing down hundreds of royal names is a difficult matter and some names may easily drop out. Moreover, the Matsya, Brahm─Бpda and V─Бyu themselves say that they 1377 mention only the prominent kings of the Ikс╣гv─Бku line and the B╚Щhadratha line 1378 and even as to the Paurava 1379 line it is clear that that dynasty had many names not all of which are enumerated. There is therefore every possibility that some kings dropped out even in later genealogies also (as for example Matsya 213. 16, saying that Andhras were 29 and V─Бgu 99.357 saying they were 30 ). Merely counting the total of the kings actually named in the Pur─Бс╣Зas would not convoy a quite accurate idea of the total length of years during which that dynasty ruled. Bearing in mind the two matters (viz, trustworthiness of tradition and Paur─Бnika genealogies and the ease of remembering the time distance between too well-known events) he ehould have endeavoured to find out the age of the Bharata War. Pargiter’s methoi criticized

able simply on the ground (which appears to me flimsy ) that the figures (1015, 1050) are discrepant (A. LH. T. p. 180). In almost all passages of the Pur─Бс╣Зas there is some discrepancy or other. Therefore, he should have made an endeavour to find out which one of the three perioda (1015, 1050, 1500 ) is supported by the best and oldest mss, and should have stuck to them, part cularly when the Sanskrit equivalents of the three periods (pa├▒cada┼Ыa, panc┼л┼Ыat and pa├▒casata) are so much alike that scribes might easily have been confused and made their own readings. Even taking the least period of (1015) years, the Mah─Бbh─Бrata War would have to be placed at about 1440 B. C. (adding 1015 to the date of Nanda’s enthronement, vize about 425 B. C.). Most Western writers and Prof. S. N. Pradhan (in ‘Chronology of ancient India’, Caloutta 1927, pp. 2497) find fault with Paur─Бс╣Зika statements and brush them aside as practically worthless. Prof. Pradhan takes the kings actually named in the three lines, holds that 28 years are the average reign period of each and multiplying the number of kings by 28 arrives at the conclusion that the Mah─Бbh─Бrata war was fought about 1150 B.O. It is not possible to deal at length with his arguments. But he ignores the express words of the Pur─Бс╣Зas that they em merate only the principal or important kings. Besides, there are scholars like Pargiter who regard 17 or 18 years as the average reign of a king in other countries (and in India also). The author cannot accept Prof. Pradhan’s reasoning. Most Western writers are loth to assign old dates to matters Indian. Pargiter is no exception. Instead of straightforwardly accepting one of the three periods that was strongly supported by mss, he in dulges in some devices that appear like trioks of jugglery (A. I. H. T. pp. 180-183). His method requires some explanation and examination.

Vy─Бsa is said to have been alive when the Bh─Бrata battle was fought at the end of the Dv─Бpara age and he is also hold to be the author of the 18 Pur─Бpas. The kings before the Bh─Бrata war, the Pandava heroes and a fow descendants of them and of some contemporaries of them are treated by the Matsya, V─Бyu, Brahm─Бnda and others as past (atila). (excluding Arjuna) is said to have been alive when the Pur─Бс╣Зas were narrated to the sages at the sattra. Both V─Бyu (99. 282) and Matsya (271.5) state that in the Ikс╣гv─Бku line king Divakara, 6th (or 5th in Matsya) in descent from Brhadbala, was alive at the time when the Pur─Бс╣Зas were narrated. Then the same Pur─Бс╣Зas (V─Бyu 99.30, Matsya 271. 23 and Brahm─Бnda III. 74. 113) state that in the line of Jar─Бgandha (ruler of Magadha ), who was a contemporary of the P─Бс╣Зdavas and whose son Sahadeva was killed in the Bh─Бrata war, there was Sen─Бjit who was a contemporary of Adhisima-krspa and Div─Бkara and who was 7th in descent from Sahadeva. These three are desoribed as vartam─Бna kings in the Pur─Бpas and all those that came after these three are described ag bhavisya. Pargiter first (AIHT p. 180) takes the total of the kings of the three dynasties, Aikс╣гv─Бka, Paurava and M─Бgadha that are actually named (ignoring what the Pur─Бс╣Зas say viz. that only the principal or important kings are named and not all) and the total of the re igns of all these (1408 years), finds that the average of the reign of each king in the three lines works out at such large figures as 47, 50, 31, which he regards as impossible when tested by real historical averages. But he forgets that the Pur─Бс╣Зas say that in the Aikс╣гv─Бka, Magadha and Peurava lines and also generally only the prominent kings are mentioned and also that the extant Pur─Бс╣Зas are only fragments left of the originals, since in the Brahm─Бnda (III. 74) all Paurava and Aikс╣гv─Бka kings are altogether lost. Then he takes the total number of kings in ten kingdoms up to Mah─Бpadma and arrives at the average of 26 kings for each kingdom (AIHT p. 181). Then he says that the average of fourteen series of kings in Eastern and Western countries which he examined comes to 19 years for each reign and, holding that the average in eastern countries is less than in western countries, he arrives at the average of 18 years for each reign (pp. 181тАУ182), which he regards as fair and rather liberal. He then multiplies the average 18 (of length of reigns ) by 26 (the average number of kings in ten countries which he supposes to be the only kings in those ten) and arrives at the figure 468 years. He adds these to the date whioh be assigns to Mah─Бpadma Nanda 38% B. C. and thus arrives at 850 B. O. as the mean date of the beginning of the reign of Adhisimakrsna, Divakara and Sep─Бj─лt, who were vartam─Бna kings. Then he takes five as the average of the kings between the vartam─Бna kings and Yudhiс╣гthira and assigning about 100 years to those 5 kings, arrives at the date 950 B, O, for the Bb─Бrata war. HoFargiter and the Bh─Бrata war

849

disoards (AIHT p. 180 n 3) the astronomical evidence contained in the Pur─Бс╣Зas (and the Mah─Бbh─Бrata) about Bharata war in a single sentence viz, that astronomical statements can have no scientific precision and can only have been formed by estimate at the close. The probable date of the Mah─Бbh─Бrata war has been discussed by the author at some length on materials supplied by the Mah─Бbb─Бrata, the Pur─Бс╣Зas, Var─Бhamihira, ─Аryabbata and Inscriptions in vol. II, pp. 895-923 and there fore he does not go into that question here. But he strongly disapproves of the methods of Pargiter and the date he deduces. Later scholars like Kirfel have not accepted the conclusions of Pargiter about two separate traditions and about the Pur─Бс╣Зas being Sanskritizations of originals written in Prakrit and Kharoс╣гthi script (p. XVI. of Intro. to ‘Pur─Бс╣Зa Texta &c.). The importance of another independent source has not been taken proper notice of by Pargiter and even by Kirfel. It appears that about 300 B.C. Megasthenes was supplied with a list of kings from Bacchus to Alexander’s tima (153 or 154 in gumber) covering by their reigns a period of 6451 years and three months. Vide ‘Ancient India as described by Megasthenes and Arrian’ by McCrindle (1877) p. 115 and Cambridge History of Ancient India, Vol. I (1922, p. 409). Even supposing for argument that the account of kings is not trustworthy the fact remains that about 300 B. C. Indians claimed that they had lists of kings that reigned before that date for thousands of years (and not for a few hundred years as Pargiter would have us believe ).

It has been shown above that Apastamba mentions a Bhavi syatpur─Бс╣Зa and quotes four verses from a Purana (p. 817, note 1328). That Bhaviс╣гyat-purana was probably so called because it contained in a prophetic voin the names and other details of kings that flourished after the Bh─Бrata heroes and after a fow generations of descendants of them and their contemporaries and probably purported to have been composed by some sage or by Vy─Бba. Pur┼лnas divide future kings in two groups

Ksatriya class), Parasayas (the Parsi tribe or parsons born of a ┼Ы┼лdra father and br─Бhmaс╣Зa mother?), S┼лdras (as kings) and others that are foreigners, the Andhrae, Sakas, Pulindas, Calikas, Yavadas, fishermen, Abhiras and Sabaras and others born of Mleccba (tribes these kings I shall proclaim one after another in order and by name. Out of these (two groups) the first is Adhisimakrс╣гpa who is now alive and I shall speak of the kings of his line that are narrated in the Bhaviс╣гya’. This passage makes it perfectly clear that in the ancient Bhaviс╣гyat kings of the three lines of Aila, Aikс╣гv─Бka and Paurava to the last of them were enumerated (vide note 1382), but that the later kings like the Andbrag and Sakas were not enumerated therein. I agree with Pargiter (p. VIII. in Intro. to ‘Pur─Бpa Toxta’ &c.) that the words ‘Bhavisye kathit─Бn’ in Matsya (50.77) or ‘Bhavisye pathit─Бn’ in V─Бyu (in 99.292) rс╣Ыfer to the descendants mentioned in the Bhaviс╣гya and that they do not simply mean ‘mentioned in future.” I fail to understand, however, why be regards ‘Bhaviс╣гyat’ as a perversion of ‘Bhaviс╣гya’. Bhaviс╣гyat is as good a word as Bhaviс╣гya, being employed in several passages such as Var─Бba ( 177, 34 ), Matsya ( 53. 62).

Pargiter probably wants to identify the Bhaviс╣гyat of Apa stamba with the Bhaviс╣гya of later times. There is no evidence except the name to identify the two. It, therefore, appears that the extant Pur─Бс╣Зas base their narrative as to the three lines of Aila, Aikс╣гv─Бka and Paurava kings on the materials contained in the ancient Bhaviс╣гya and as to other lines and compara tively later kings they relied on other materials or oral traditions that they could collect. This inference receives support from other circumstances. The extant Pur─Бс╣Зas quote versos called Anuyamsa slokas or gathas about ancient kings, such as K─Бrta virya (in V─Бyu 94. 20, Matsya 43, 24, Brahm─Бnda III. 68-20, Brahma 13.17) and also about the last scions of the Alla and Aiksv─Бka lines, viz. Sumitra and Ksemaka. But so far as kings of comparatively later dynasties such as those of the Andbras, Sungas and others are concerned, no such g─Бth─Бs or slokas are quoted in the Pur─Бс╣Зas. There is absolutely no evidence to hold, as Pargiter doas (p. XIII of Pur─Бс╣Зa Texts’ &o.), that the ancient Bhaviс╣гya contained & reference to the Guptas. The ancient Bhaviс╣гya was composed before ─Аpastamba (ie, before the 4th or 5th B.O.) and hence originally could have contained no reference to the Guptas (whose rule began about 320 A. D.). The Matsya does not refer to the Guptas and mentions only the

852

History of Dharmaj─Бstra (Soc. IV, Oh XXII

downfall of the Andhras. Therefore, the Matsya should be regard ed as composed or revised about the middle (or end) of the Srd century A. D., though the possibilities of some chapters or versos being added after that date cannot be ruled out. When the Vayu (99. 383), Brahm─Бnda (III. 74. 195), Viс╣гс╣Зu (IV. 24. 18) and Bhagavata (XII. 1. 37) mentioned the Guptas as rulers the first two probably added these passages just about the time when Gupta rule began and the Viс╣гс╣Зu and Bh─Бgavata (which present a corrupt text) might bave borrowed the information from mss. of V─Бyu or Brahm─Бnda. It is clear, however, that the first two (out of the four) Pur─Бс╣Зas were composed or revised about 320-335 A. D. and the other two later still.

Kirfel’s work тАШPur─Бс╣Зa Pancalaksana’ is one of fundamental importance so far as the Pur─Бс╣Зas are concerned, since it adopts & new method for the treatment of Paur─Бс╣Зika material. The German Introduction of this work has been reproduced in English in the Journal of the Shri Venkatesa Institute at Tirupati, in yol. VII, pp. 81тАУ121 and vol VIII. pp. 9-33. Kirfel disagrees with many of the views of Pargiter. His main con clusions are: Apart from the abridgemont in Agni and Garuс╕Нa as well as the prose paraphrase in Viс╣гс╣Зu, there are only three completo groups of Pur─Бpa texts viz. Brahma and Harivamsa, Brahmaс╣Зda and V─Бyu, and that of Matsya, all other Pur─Бс╣Зas containing only smaller or larger parts of the same. Of the three groups, Brahm─Бnda and Hariyamsa are the oldest ( and not Brah m─Бpda and Vayu as Pargiter in A.I.H.T. p. 78 says). Kirfel holds that the Brahm─Бnda and Vayu must have originally been a single Pur─Бс╣Зa, particularly because the largest parts of both agree with each other, that Pargiter is not right in thinking that the additions in V─Бyu and Brahm─Бnda were borrowed from the ancient Bhaviс╣гya (Kirfel p. 18, vol. VII. of J. V. O. I. p. 92), but that the borrowed material goes back to an independent text. Kirfel does not accept Pargiter’s theory that the Pur─Бс╣Зas were Sanskritizations of Prakrit texts, that the Viс╣гс╣Зu in its existing form is a younger Pur─Бс╣Зa than the V─Бyu or Brabr─Бnda in spite of the fact that it most faithfully observes the basic arrangement of the five characteristics of Pur─Бс╣Зas. The division of Pur─Бс╣Зas into 18 and the distribution of Pur─Бс╣Зas into s─Бttvika, r─Бjasa and t─Бmasa are not original items but are applicable only to the last definitive texts of the Pur─Бс╣Зas. Pargiter thought that there existed an Ur-Pur─Бpa which had treated of the five topios (of sarga &c.) in ideal completeness and clear disposition Kirfel

Kirfel and Pargiter

859

says that this is soarooly more than an arbitrary assumption (p. XL VIII of Kirfel’s Intro. and J. V.O. I. vol. VIII, p. 31).

The present author may tentatively accept most of the con clusions of Kirfel, but he differs, as stated above from Kirfel’s view that the five characteristic topics ( sarga &c.) are the oldest constituent parts of the whole Pur─Бс╣Зa Literature.

A lengthy discussion of the age of the Pur─Бpas is not very relevant to the subject of this section. But it would not be entirely out of place if the author said a few words thereon.

The author’s position about the Pur─Бс╣Зas is as follows: We know hardly anything about the Pur─Бс╣Зa mentioned in Atharva veda, the Satapatha and the ancient Upaniс╣гads; but this much is clear that Purana had attained a status of sacredness like the Vedas and was closely associated with Itih─Бsa even in Vedic times. This is the first stage in the evolution of Pur─Бс╣Зa Literg ture, but we know nothing about the contents of the Pur─Бс╣Зa in those ancient times. The Tai. ─А, mentions Pur─Бn─Бni, therefore in its time there must have been three Pur─Бс╣Зas at least. As ─Аp. Dh. 8. quotes four verses from a Pur─Бс╣Зa and expressly names Bhaviс╣гyatpur─Бс╣Зa, it follows that by the 5th or 4th century B. C. at the latest, there was in existence a Bhaviс╣гyat-purana and other Pur─Бс╣Зas or a Pur─Бс╣Зa, that contained sarga and pratisarga and some Smrti material. This we may regard as the 2nd definite stage of Purana Literature, of the contents of which we have some traces at least.

The Mah─Бbh─Бrata quotes hundreds of versos (called slokas, gathas, anuvainsa ┼Ыlokas), some of which have a bearing upon Paur─Бс╣Зika subjects and have a Paur─Бс╣Зika ring. Some examples may be cited. The Vanaparva 1384 quotes two verses about the spiritual prowess of Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra and about his assertion that he was a br─Бhmaс╣Зa. Stages in the growth of Puranas

855

or 6th century A. D. to the 9th century A. D. This represents the 4th stage in the evolution of Pur─Бс╣Зa literature. The Upe pur─Бс╣Зas began to be compiled from about the 7th or 8th century A. D. and their numbers went on increasing till about the 13th century A. D. or even later. This is the last phase. Thus there is enough evidence to hold that the Pur─Бс╣Зas began to influence Hindu society a few centuries before Christ, that their influence continued in full force till the 17th or 18th century A. D. and that it continues to some extent oven now. After the 9th century no further Mah─Бpur─Бс╣Зas appear, but additional matter appears to have been unscrupulously inserted in several Pur─Бс╣Зas, the worst example of the kind being the third part of the Bhaviс╣гya, which contains stories of Adam and Eve, of Pithviraj and Jaicandra, Taimur, Akbar, Caitanya, Bhattoji, Nadirshah and so on.

The word ‘pur─Бpa’ occurs over a dozen times in the Rgveda, is an adjective and means ‘ancient, old’. The Nigbantu (III. 27) mentions six Vedic words as having the sense of ‘pur─Бpa’ viz. pratnam, pradivaс╕е, pravay─Бс╕е, sanemi, p┼лrvyan, ahn─Бya. Yaska (Nirukta III, 19) derives the word ‘purana’ as ‘pur─Б navam bbavati’ (what was now in former times ). The Rgveda does not contain the word ‘pur─Бtana’ (ancient). Pur─Бс╣Зa may be a very old form of pur─Бtana’ through the intermediate form ‘pur─Бapa.’ From meaning ancient the word ‘pur─Бс╣Зa’came to mean a work dealing with ancient tales; it became a noun and was applied in the times of the Atharvaveda, the Satapatha and the Upaniс╣гads to a class of works containing ancient tales. When pur─Бс╣Зa came to mean a work dealing with ancient tales, to speak of a Bhaviс╣гyat-pur─Бс╣Зa was apparently a contradiction in terms. That contradiction was probably not minded or was ignored by the thought that works that narrated old tales gradually came to inolude comparatively rocont ones and bad therefore to adopt & prophetic style of composition with reference to the latter.

The V─Бyu 1388 derives the word ‘Pur─Бс╣Зa’ from ‘pur─Б’ (in ancient times, formerly) and the root ‘an’ (to breathe or live), and therefore according to it the word literally means that which lives in the past’ or that which breathes ancient times’,

The Padmapur─Бс╣Зa propounds & slightly different etymology, viz.” it is called Pur─Бс╣Зa because it desires or likes the past” from ‘pur─Б’ and the root vas’ ( to desire or to like).

The question why the extant Pur─Бс╣Зas do not narrate the traditions about the dynasties of the Guptas and their successors cannot be satisfactorily answered. One reason may be that the original kernel of some Pur─Бс╣Зas like the extant Matsya were compiled before the Guptas rose to power, while others like the V─Бyu and Brahm─Бnda were compiled while the Gupta rule was in its infancy. Another reason may have been that in the 5th and following centuries when many of the extant Pur─Бс╣Зas were compiled northern India was very much disturbed by the invasions of foreigners like the H┼лnas, 1389 Toram─Бna and Mihirakula, numerous sects and schisms had arisen, Buddhism had become powerful and therefore the first task of the intelligent and devout followers of the Vada was held to be to wean the common people away from schisms like that of Buddha, to lay down the foundations of a new ideology among the masses and to emphasize and assimilate as many of the doctrines of the sects and schisms as possible with their ancient traditions and practices. The intelligent classes, therefore, emphasized the importance of such virtues as ahims─Б, satya, bhakti and of vratas, pilgrimages, ┼Ыr─Бddhas and d─Бnas and were probably not in a mood to record the names of foreign conquerors or of small chieftains fighting with each other and unable to repel the cruel invaders. For the absence of references to the dynasties of the Guptas and their successors, Pargiter blames the br─Бhmaс╣Зas in the following characteristic passage (AIHT chap. 4 p. 57) ’the absolute dearth of traditional history after that stage is quite intelligible, both because the compilation of the Purana had set a goal of tradition and because the Purana soon passed into the hands of br─Бhmanas who preserved what they had received, but with the brahmanio lack of the historio sense, added nothing about the later kings’. Supposing for argument that br─Бhmaс╣Зas lacked the historic sense, Pargitor’s opinion appears to be entirely one-sided. He assigns no reason why the g┼лtas, whose business was to record and preserve historic tradition ( as he himself says on p. 58 of AIHT), did not stick to their business and did not continue to compose gonealogios of

The legends about Vy─Бsa and S┼лta may be briefly considered. The Pur─Бс╣Зas declare that Vy─Бsa was the son of Par─Б┼Ыara, was also called K╚Щspa Dvaip─Бyana and was an incarnation of Viс╣гс╣Зu ( of Brahm─Б also in Vayu 77. 74тАУ75 and of ┼Ъiva in the Kurma II. 11. 136). History of Dharmat─Бstra (Soo. IV, Ch. XXtt

that no one should teach or repeat in japa or employ & mantra in a sacrifice without knowing the rс╣гi, the metre, the deity and the Use (viniyoga) of it and dire consequences were declared to follow for him who was remiss in these matters. Hymns and mantras were arranged in different groups for different religi ous rites and solemn sacrifices or for other purposes (such as ┼Ы─Бntis). It is not necessary to remember who arranged the required mantras for rites, sacrifices and other purposes. The Br─Бhmaс╣Зa texts and srauta s┼лtras prescribe the manner of the employment of the same mantras for various purposes and the Anukramaс╣Зis contain the names of the seors, metres and the deities of hymns and individual mantras also. As every mantra of the Veda was supposed to be only seen by a sage and as eternal, the more compilation of them in one or more series of the more arrangement of mantras or hymns in different groups for different purposes did not at all affect or interfere with the eternality of mantras or hymns. Pargiter’s 80-called reason for suppressing the name of the arranger of the Veda is simply do reason at all.

Pargiter did not stop to consider possible explanations. One of the most plausible is now put forth. The Mah─Бbh─Бrata and the Purapas (a very extensive literature) were attributed to Vy─Бsa, who, as shown above in n. 1390, had come to be looked upon as Viapu or as an avat─Бra of Vispu. The four Vedas and the Beveral different ┼Ы─Бkh─Бs (recensions) of each Veda were well known, By a sort of post facto explanation, the distribution of the Veda into four main groups was claimed to have been brought about by the divinely inspired Vy─Бsa, whose Puranas are, as will be apparent from note 1349 above and as will be shown in more detail later, glorified as even anterior and superior to the Veda. The eternality of the Veda had to be maintained and at the same time Vy─Бsa was to be glorified.

How Vyasa was proclaimed as Veda-arranger

861

The easiest way was to proclaim that Vy─Бsa, the author of the great Epio and of the distribution of Pur─Бс╣Зas into eighteen, was also responsible for the division or arrangement of the Veda. If all this glorification of Vy─Бga occurred in the centuries immediately preceding and following Christ, how could the supposed arranger of the Veda be mentioned in the early Vedic Literature, which, most scholars agree, was closed some centuries before Buddha (i.e. before the 6th century B. C.)? No body claims that the arrangement into mandalas or astakas or k─Бс╣Зdas is eternal. It is only the hymns or mantras that are claimed to be eternal. Even the padap─Бtha of the Rgveda is declared to be non-etornal and is ascribed to S─Бkalya whom tbe Nirakta criticises in VI, 28. Vi┼Ыvar┼лpa on Y─Бj. III. 242 expressly states that the pada and krama arrangements of the Veda are due to human authors.1394 This theory explains all matters and has far better claims to be accepted than Pargiter’s bold ascription of fraudulent suppression inferred from (& suppogod) silence.

The Pur─Бс╣Зas do not speak with one voice about their own origin and transmission. After declaring that Vy─Бsa entrusted the preservation and propagation of the Puranas to S┼лta, the V─Бyu and other Pur─Бс╣Зas contain a somewhat different version. The V─Бyu narrates (61. 55-61): S┼лta had six disciples, vis, Suniti Atreya, Akstavraс╣Зa K─Б┼Ыyapa, Agnivarcas Bh─Бrdvaja, Mitrayu Vasiс╣гtha, S─Бvarс╣Зi Saumadatti, Su┼Ыarman S─Бm┼Ыap─Бyana. Three of these, viz. K─Б┼Ыyapa, S─Бvarpi and S─Бmsap─Бyana prepared new Purana-aambit─Бs and S┼лta’s own was the 4th and the original one. All were divided into four k─Бndas, contained the name sense (matter), but differed in their readings as the s┼лkhas of the Veda differ. All had four thousand verses except tho namhita of Samsap─Бyana. These four are said to be the basic saribit─Бs (in Brahm─Бс╣Зda II. 35. 66 ) or original samhitas (in V─Бyu 61, 58 p┼лrvasambit─Бs). The Brahm─Бnda (II. 35. 63тАУ70) has the same account in almost in the same words. Viс╣гс╣Зu (IL. 6. 16-17), Agni 271 (11-12) are briefer but agree in the main with Vayu. The Bhagav─Бta (XII. 7.467) differs from all these to some extent. That there is some substance in this story appears from stray passages in several chapters of the Vayu (56.1, 60. 33-34, 62. 1, 89. 16) and the Brahmapda (IL. 34, 34,

One need not accept the divine character of S┼лta, one has only to understand that in very ancient times br─Бhmaс╣Зas could, without any qualms and with out loss of prestige, learn about legends from a s┼лta, but that in the times when the extant pur─Бс╣Зas were compiled the position had entirely changed.

History of Dharmad─Бstra ( Seo. IV, Oh. XXII

in question Prof. Hazra quotes & passage from 1403 Saс╣Еkari d─Бrya’s bh─Бsya on Chandogya III. 4. 1-2 and completely misun derstands the great ac─Бrya when he observes тАЬ┼Ъhaс╣Еkara’s use of the word ‘r─Бtri’ in the plural (id ‘r─Бtriс╣гu’) shows that in his opinion the Itih─Бsa and Pur─Бс╣Зa were employed every night during the P─Бriplava and not merely on the sth and oth nights respectively, as the Satapatha Br. and S─Бс╣Еkh─Бyana┼Ыrautasutra say” (Italios author’s). The A┼Ыvamedha sacrifice lasted for a year and listening to the P─Бriplava went on for a year, each P─Бripla va being a cycle of ten days (or rather nights, as the recitation by the hotr priest was to take place after the morning, mid-day and evening istis to Savitr were finished). The texts to be recited and the nature of the legends to be Darrated on each of the cycle of ten days are fixed and Itih─Бsa and Pur─Бс╣Зa are to be recited only on the 8th and 9th nights. As each cycle was of ten days, there would be 36 cycles of P─Бriplava in a year and Itihasa would be recited on 36 nights in the year and Pur─Бс╣Зa also would be recited on 36 nights in the year. It is for this reason that Saс╣Еkar─Бc─Бrya speaks of *P─Бriplav─Бsu r─Бtriс╣гu’ in the plural, but he does not say that Itih─Бsa and Pur─Бpa were to be recited on all’ nights (sarr─Бsu r─Бtriс╣гu), while Prof. Hazra represents him as so saying. There is absolutely no warrant in the ancient texts for saying that on each day (or night) of the P─Бriplava Itih─Бsa or Pur─Бс╣Зa was to be recited or that Saс╣Еkar─Бc─Бrya said anything of the kind. The testimony of the Ved─Бntas┼лtra 1404 is completely against this view of Prof. Hazra. Prof. Hazra and Prof. Dikshitar on Pur─Бс╣Зas

of king Manu Vaivasvata (which was to be recited on the first night of the Pariplava).

Prof. Hazra has recently published (in 1958) Studies in the Upapur─Бс╣Зas’ vol. I pp. 1-400 on Saura and Vaiс╣гс╣Зava Upa pur─Бс╣Зas (in the Calcutta Sanskrit College Research Series 1958). This would be briefly dealt with a little later.

Prof. Ramchandra Dikshitar also has written a good deal on the Pur─Бс╣Зas. His writings are boset by the same infirmity that attaches to Prof. Hazra’s work to some extent. For example, in & paper published in the Proceedings of the 13th Indian History Congress (pp. 46-50) on the Viс╣гс╣Зupur─Бс╣Зa he first states (p. 46) that he is more concerned with the extant Vispupur─Бс╣Зa and after pointing out that the topics of vratas, of fasts, of tirtha, are absent from the extant Vispupur─Бс╣Зa he concludes that the extant Viс╣гс╣Зupur─Бс╣Зa can safely be placed in the 6th or 7th century B. C. Hardly any modern and critical scholar would accept such a date for the extant Viс╣гс╣Зupur─Бс╣Зa. Instead of relying on the absence of certain topics he should rather have relied on what it actually contains to find out the probable date of the. extant Viс╣гс╣Зupur─Бс╣Зa.

In connection with the Pur─Бс╣Зas the author must refer at some length to the Introductory remarks of Ballalagona, king of Bengal, in his D─Бnasagara, edited in the B. I series (1953-1956 ) by Mr. Bhabatosh Bhattac─Бrya (three parts of text pp. 1-722 and 4th part an Introduction in English with Indices). Those remarks evince a bold critical faculty rare in our medieval Sanskrit writers. He mentions, besides the Gopathabr─Бhmaс╣Зa, the R─Бm─Бyaс╣Зa, the Mah─Бbh─Бrata, the smrtis and dharma┼Ы─Бstras of Gautama, Manu, Y─Бjnavalkya, (count ing Saс╣Еkha Likhita ag two ), D─Бna-Bс╣Ыhaspati and Bс╣Ыhaspati (as separate), Vasiс╣гtha and others (in all 28), the Chandoga-pari ┼Ыiс╣гс╣нa of K─Бty─Бyana, thirteen principal Pur─Бс╣Зas viz. Br─Бhma, V─Бr─Бba, Agneya, Bhaviс╣гya, M─Бtsya, V─Бmana, V─Бyaviya, M─Бrkandeya, Vaiс╣гс╣Зava, ┼Ыaiva, Sk─Бnda, P─Бdma and Kaurma and the Upapur─Бс╣Зas named in K┼лrma and Adi Pur─Бс╣Зas as contain: ing the procedure of various ) d─Бnas, viz. ─Аdya, Samba, K─Бlik─Б, N─Бnda (v.1 Nandin), Aditya, Narasimha, Viс╣гс╣Зudharmottara (declared by M─Бrkandeya) and the sastra called Viс╣гс╣Зudharina (eight in all). Some important conclussions can be drawn from the above mentioned remarks of Ballalasena in his D─Бnas─Бgara. Next to the Mitakс╣гar─Б, the Kс╣Ыtyakalpataru and Apar─Бrka’s commentary, the D─Бnas─Бgara is among the earliest extant nibandha works the dates of which are nearly certain. 1407 If does not mention the Mit─Бksar─Б, nor Kr╠еtyakalpataru nor Apar─Бrka. Mr. Bhabatosh Bhatt─Бcb─Бrya is right in bis contention that the words ‘Kalpa drumo jangamah’ in the 3rd opening vorge have nothing to do with Kalpataru of Lakс╣гmidhara and that Prof. Rangaswamy Aiyangar is wrong in thinking that the verse refers to the Kalpataru ( vide Mr. Bhatt─Бcb─Бrya’s Introduction to Danas─Бgara p. The principal points that emerge from Ball─Бlasena’s remarks on Pur─Бс╣Зas are that he included both V─Бyu and ┼Ъiva among the principal Pur─Бс╣Зas (sometimes called Mah─Бpur─Бс╣Зas), that there were two Pur─Бс╣Зas called Linga, Brahma, ─Аgney& and Viс╣гс╣Зu, that the four counterparts bearing these names were not treated as authoritative (one pseudo-Lingapur─Бс╣Зa having 6000 verses and one Vispu having also 23000 verses being unautho ritative), that he abhorred Tantrik rites and therefore totally discarded the Devipur─Бс╣Зa and parts of Bhaviс╣гya, that he did not utilize three named sections of the Skanda, that he did not regard the Garuс╕Нa as authoritative. I shall discuss the question of Viс╣гс╣Зudharma pur─Бс╣Зa later. But I have serious objections against the antiquity and authenticity of this passage. Being quoted in the Kalpataru it may be earlier than 1050 A. D. That is all. In order to glorify the 18 Pur─Бс╣Зas the meaning of Jaya is extended. Jaya is applied only to the Mah─Бbh─Бrata in the latter e.g. Udyogaparva 136. 18-19 and Svarg─Бrohanika 5. 49 and 51 quoted in note 1369 above. Hence this passage was inserted very late after all 18 Pur─Бс╣Зas had been composed i.e. after the 9th century AD. Besides the plural Viс╣гс╣Зudharm─Бdi┼Ы─Бstr─Бс╣Зi’ shows that many works are meant and not one, i.e. the meaning is that all ┼Ы─Бstras dealing with Viс╣гс╣Зudharmas and the like. If a single work were meant, one expects ‘Viс╣гс╣Зudharm─Бdi┼Ы─Бstram ca’ and that would have not spoilt the metre. Besides, the Kalpataru itself indioates that this verse about ‘Jaya’ was cited by some authorities as * Smrti’. Therefore, it is doubtful if it is a genuine Bhaviс╣гya passage. Ball─Бlasena mentions only eight Upapur─Бс╣Зas on danas by name (including the four mentioned by the Matsya).

In spite of the very admirable and praiseworthy efforts of Dr. Hazra in the matter of the place of the Upapur─Бс╣Зas, their contents, the search among numerous mss. to find out what the text of the several apapur─Бс╣Зas has been, the present writer must say here once for all that he does not at all agree with most of the dates that he assign to the S─Бmba, the Vispadharma, the Viс╣гс╣Зudharmottara, the Narasimhapur─Бс╣Зa, which are the principal Upapur─Бс╣Зas he has dealt with in the first volume of ‘Studies in Upapur─Бс╣Зas’. Samba between 500 and 800 A..D. (p. 91), the Viс╣гс╣Зudharma pur─Бс╣Зa between 200-300 A. D. (p. 143), the Viс╣гс╣Зudharmottara pur─Бpa between 400 to 500 A. D. (p. 212) and the present Narasimha-pur─Бс╣Зa between 400 to 500 A. D. To examine all his reasons the present writer would have to write another book. Therefore, he proposes to give only a few illustrations of the way in which Dr. Hazra arrives at his dates. But before this is done it is better to mention some of his own findings on the Pur─Бс╣Зas and the four Upapur─Бс╣Зas mentioned above. On p. 27 he observes that the text of the extant Mah─Бpur─Бс╣Зas which are the results of innumerable changes, modifications and interpola tions made at different times and by different sects is scarcely reliable and can be used with great caution and careful discrimination. I agree with him. But the same or perhaps worse is the case with the Upapur─Бс╣Зas. Prof. Hazra himself says (“Studies’ vol. I. p. 23) that after the group of 18 principal Pur─Бс╣Зas had been compiled many sub-systems and sects like the S─Бktas and Sauras came into prominence and their adherents interpolated chapters in the 18 established Pur─Бс╣Зas and wrote new and independent works styled Pur─Бс╣Зas in order to propagate their own ideas and that some of these latter came to be called Upapur─Бс╣Зas. The result is that, unless we have critical editions of the Pur─Бс╣Зas and the principal Upapur─Бс╣Зas on the model of the critical edition of the Mah─Бbh─Бrata at the BORI in Poona, all chapters and often single verges are suspect. But the task of preparing critical editions of oven the principal Pur─Бс╣Зas and some of the Upapur─Бс╣Зas based upon ancient and medieval mss. collected from all parts of India would be far more colossal and costly than even the critical edition of the Mah─Бbh─Бrata. Therefore, most chronological conclusions about the dates of Pur─Бс╣Зas and Upapur─Бс╣Зas and about the borrowings of one Purana from another are just tentative at the most and likely to be set aside by new evidence as long as oritical editions of Puranas and Upapur─Бс╣Зas are not available.

Let us now turn to the four Upapur─Бс╣Зas dealt with at longth by Prof. Hazra. About the Samba (which is one of the four Upapur─Бс╣Зas expressly named in the Matsyapur─Бс╣Зa 53. 60_63) Prof. Hazra observes (“Studies’ vol. There is at present only one printed ed. of the Samba viz. that of the Vonk. Press in 84 chapters based probably only on one ms. Out of these 84 chapters Prof. Hazra himself finds that 47 chapters are later than 950 A. D., of which 4% were added between 1250-1500 A. D. Prof. Hazra has himaself examined several mass of the Samba, but they do not come from all over India, many being from Bengal and almost all seem to be late ones (p. 33 last line), being copied in saka 1764 i. e. 1842 A, D. When more than half of this Purana ranges between 950 to 1500 AD. according to Prof. Hazra, how can it be usefully employed for chronological purposes? Nobody can say when the verses about the four Upapur─Бс╣Зas were inserted in tbe Matsya, but one can affirm that it was done about the 9th century A. D. or even later. Two datos about Upapur─Бс╣Зas are certain, viz. that Samba is mentioned by Alberuni (Bachau, I. p. 130) who wrote in 1030 ├Е. Then we come to the Vizpudharmottara. The only printed edition is that of the Venk. Press. It is a vast work divided into three sections. The first deals with geography, solar and lunar dynasties, astronomy and astrology, gotra and pravara, ┼Ыr─Бddhas, Manvantaras, Bharata’s fight with Gandharvas and Satrughoa’s with Lavana. In ohap. 53 a fow exploits of Balarama and Krsna are narrated in the same chapter and so the half line was inserted later. As the story of Buddha is not given Prof. Hazra holds that the verse in 36. 9 is undoubtedly spurious (pp. 230 and 249). It never occurred to Prof. Hazra that the sectarian zeal of & bigoted Vaiс╣гс╣Зava might have never cared to give the life of Buddha who was not concerned with (but was antagonistic to ) the varpa system and the Vedas or might have omitted the story of the life of Buddha even if it oocurred in the ms. from which he copied. The prosent author thinks that 36.9 is a genuine verse and enumerates Buddha as an avat─Бra following the universal belief held in India at least from the 9th or 10th century A. D. that Buddha was an avat─Бra, but probably bigotry led to the omission of the details of Buddha’s life. Besides, the verse is capable of another interpretation. It is only the stories of ten avat─Бras (excluding Buddha) that lead & devoted to Visgu. A devotee may worship any one of Nс╣Ыsimba, R─Бma or Krona, and reach Vispu, but hardly any orthodox work has ever stated that worship of Buddha alone (an avatara) will load to attainment of Viс╣гс╣Зu. Therefore, it is proper to hold that the story of Buddha’s life might not have been given at all or was purposely omitted. On. p. 239 Prof. Hasra pats forward a peculiar argument. some mes. Prof. Hazra’s judgments on various matters are most liable to be challenged; but considerations of space prevent any detailed treatment. On p. 252 he holds that in chap. 6 the story of the birth of Vasistha and Agastya as the song of Mitra and Varuс╣Зa, the story of Yana and Yaml are comparatively late additions. The story of the birth of Vasistha from Urvasi and Mitra and Varuс╣Зa is alluded to even in the Rgveda ( VII. 33. 11 ) and in the previous verse ( 10 ) of the same hymn Vasistha, Agastya and Mitr─Бvarunau are brought together. The Pur─Бс╣Зa may have added some fringes and embellishments, but the main theme is the same in both. On p. 254тАУ255 and note 330 Prof. Hazra was constrained to admit that the Narasimha pur─Бpa had been revised more than onco, remarks that Hem─Бdri had a more extensive pur─Бс╣Зa before him than the printed one and that a comparison of the verges quoted from the Narasimha pur─Бс╣Зa by the Madanap─Бrij─Бta pp. 301-302, Smrtitattva on ─Бhnika (vol. The extant Narasimhapur─Бс╣Зa is an insignificant one as compared with the principal Pur─Бс╣Зas like V─Бyu, Matsya, Vispu; the S─Бmba is not quoted at all by Apar─Бrka, the Viс╣гс╣Зudharma only twice and even the Viс╣гс╣Зudharmottara is quoted by Apar─Бrka only 7 times for about 25 perses in all. Prof. Hazra fails to realize the full force of his own admission that the Narasimha was revised several times and that writers of the 16th century had a version before them different from that before the writers of the 13th or 14th century. In the author’s opinion the whole Fur─Бс╣Зa becomes suspect and that by mere diplomatio criticism one would not be able to support beyond doubt any chronological conclusion derived from the present truncated and garbled version of the original Narasimhan

So much space had to be devoted to an examination of Prof. Hazra’s work on the Upapur─Бс╣Зas for cogent reasons. His is the latest and most elaborate treatment of Pur─Бс╣Зas and parti cularly of Upapur─Бс╣Зas, on which he has bestowed immense labour. His opinions, particularly about the dates of the Upapur─Бс╣Зas, are likely to be taken as the last word on the subject and even his unfounded surmises are likely to be taken as well-established conclusions. Modern Sanskrit scholars have sad experience about such matters. Max Muller, working back from the date of Buddha and assigning arbitrarily two hundred years each for three periods viz. the Upaniс╣гad period, the Br─Бhmaс╣Зa period and the Samhit─Б period, inferred that the composition of the Vedas was to be assigned to a period about 1200 B. C. Ho no doubt said that his periods were the minimum and that his was a pure surmise. Yet most writers who have not themselves made a deep study of the Veda assign the Vedas to about 1200 to 1400 B. O, even after the lapse of about one hundred years from the time when Max Muller launched his surmises. For example, Prof. Toynbee in the table of the time spans of the growth phases of civilizations on p. 758 of volume IX (1954) gives the dates of Epiphany and of breakdown of the Indic civilization as 1375 B.C. and 725 B. O. rospectively. The present author does not at all agree with the datos assigned to the Upapur─Бс╣Зas by Prof. Hazra and with the methods and reasoning adopted for arriving at those dates. He had to perform this painful duty even in spite of his regard

for Prof. Hazra.

not quote much from the Puranas, but Apar─Бrka and Kalpataru quote them profusely. The Kalpataru quotes about 600 verses from Dovipurana, over 200 verses each from Kalika, Aditya pur─Бс╣Зa, Nandipur─Бpa and Narasimhapur─Бс╣Зa (all Upapuranas) while it quotes none at all from Viс╣гс╣Зudbarmottara. The Kalpataru did not probably regard it as authoritative, though Apar─Бrka and the D─Бnas─Бgara utilized it to some extent. The learnod editor of the extensive Kalpataru, Prof. Aiyangar, has made great efforts to identify the quotations therein from the Pur─Бс╣Зas, and laid all workers and scholars under great obliga tions. But many quotations have eluded him as will be shown later on. He has been assiduous in pointing out how Hem─Бdri, Cande┼Ыvara and Mitrami┼Ыra have copied wholesale’ from the Kalpataru. It is not impossible that even the Kalpataru might have done the same to some extent as regards its predecessors such as the P─Бrij─Бta, Prak─Б┼Ыa, Smrtima├▒jari, and Kamadhenu. But as those works are not yet available, no positive conclusion can be drawn about its borrowing for the present

The present author edited 286 verses from the R─Бjam─Бrtaс╣Зс╕Нa (containing 1462 verses) on tithis, vratas and utgavas in ABORI YOL 36, parts III-IV, 1956, pp. 306-339,).
